<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2014 46:3"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-01-19"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Doil Choi and colleagues report the genome sequence of the hot pepper, Capsicum annuum, as well as the resequencing of two cultivated peppers and a wild species, Capsicum chinense. Comparative genomic analysis across Solanaceae provides insights into genome expansion, pungency, ripening and disease resistance in hot peppers. Hot pepper (Capsicum annuum), one of the oldest domesticated crops in the Americas, is the most widely grown spice crop in the world. We report whole-genome sequencing and assembly of the hot pepper (Mexican landrace of Capsicum annuum cv. CM334) at 186.6&#215; coverage. We also report resequencing of two cultivated peppers and de novo sequencing of the wild species Capsicum chinense. The genome size of the hot pepper was approximately fourfold larger than that of its close relative tomato, and the genome showed an accumulation of Gypsy and Caulimoviridae family elements. Integrative genomic and transcriptomic analyses suggested that change in gene expression and neofunctionalization of capsaicin synthase have shaped capsaicinoid biosynthesis. We found differential molecular patterns of ripening regulators and ethylene synthesis in hot pepper and tomato. The reference genome will serve as a platform for improving the nutritional and medicinal values of Capsicum species."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Hot pepper (<i>Capsicum annuum</i>), one of the oldest domesticated crops in the Americas, is the most widely grown spice crop in the world. We report whole-genome sequencing and assembly of the hot pepper (Mexican landrace of <i>Capsicum annuum</i> cv. CM334) at 186.6Ã coverage. We also report resequencing of two cultivated peppers and <i>de novo</i> sequencing of the wild species <i>Capsicum chinense</i>. The genome size of the hot pepper was approximately fourfold larger than that of its close relative tomato, and the genome showed an accumulation of <i>Gypsy</i> and Caulimoviridae family elements. Integrative genomic and transcriptomic analyses suggested that change in gene expression and neofunctionalization of capsaicin synthase have shaped capsaicinoid biosynthesis. We found differential molecular patterns of ripening regulators and ethylene synthesis in hot pepper and tomato. The reference genome will serve as a platform for improving the nutritional and medicinal values of <i>Capsicum</i> species.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Hot pepper is a member of the Solanaceae family. It is a diploid, facultative, self-pollinating crop and is closely related to potato, tomato, eggplant, tobacco and petunia. Solanaceae plants belong to the asterid clade of eudicots, which includes more than 3,000 diverse species worldwide. Many members of the Solanacea family have the same number of chromosomes (<i>n</i> = 12) yet differ drastically in genome size. Hot pepper is one of the oldest domesticated crops in the Western Hemisphere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Aguilar-MelÃ©ndez, A., Morrell, P.L., Roose, M.L. &amp; Kim, S.C. Genetic diversity and structure in semiwild and domesticated chiles (Capsicum annuum; Solanaceae) from Mexico. Am. J. Bot. 96, 1190â1202 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1626">1</a></sup>, is the most widely grown spice in the world and is a major ingredient in most global cuisines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bosland, P.W.E.J.V.P. Vegetable and Spice Capsicums (CABI, Wallingford, UK, 2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1630">2</a></sup>. Hot pepper has a wide variety of uses, including in pharmaceuticals, natural coloring agents and cosmetics, as an ornamental plant and as the active ingredient in most defense repellents. Hot pepper provides many essential vitamins, minerals and nutrients that have great importance for human health<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="MarÃ­n, A., Ferreres, F., Tomas-Barberan, F.A. &amp; Gil, M.I. Characterization and quantitation of antioxidant constituents of sweet pepper (Capsicum annuum L.). J. Agric. Food Chem. 52, 3861â3869 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1634">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Matsufuji, H., Ishikawa, K., Nunomura, O., Chino, M. &amp; Takeda, M. Oxidant content of different coloured sweet peppers, white, green, yellow, orange and red (Capsicum annuum L.). Int. J. Food Sci. Technol. 42, 1482â1488 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1637">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Matus, Z., Deli, J. &amp; Szabolcs, J.J. Carotenoid composition of yellow pepper during ripeningâisolation of Î²-cryptoxanthin 5,6-epoxide. J. Agric. Food Chem. 39, 1907â1914 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1640">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Mejia, L.A., Hudson, E., deMejia, E.G. &amp; Vazquez, F. Carotenoid content and vitamin-A activity of some common cultivars of Mexican peppers (Capsicum annuum) as determined by HPLC. J. Food Sci. 53, 1448â1451 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1643">6</a></sup>. In 2011, the top 20 pepper-producing countries grew 33.3 million tons of hot pepper planted on 3.8 Mha (United Nations Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO) statistics; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Sec20">URLs</a>). In the last decade, world production of hot pepper increased by 40%.</p><p>The pungency of hot pepper is due to the accumulation of capsaicinoids, a group of alkaloids that are unique to the <i>Capsicum</i> genus. The heat sensation created by these capsaicinoids is such a defining aspect of this crop that the genus name <i>Capsicum</i> comes from the Greek <i>kapto</i>, which means 'to bite'. Capsaicin, dihydrocapsaicin and nordihydrocapsaicin constitute the primary capsaicinoids, which are produced exclusively in glands on the placenta of the fruit. The organoleptic sensation of heat caused when capsaicin binds to the mammalian transient receptor potential vanilloid 1 (TRPV1) receptor in the pain pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Caterina, M.J. et al. The capsaicin receptor: a heat-activated ion channel in the pain pathway. Nature 389, 816â824 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1662">7</a></sup> can be argued to be a sixth taste along with sweet, sour, bitter, salty and umami (savory). Many enzymes involved in capsaicinoid biosynthesis are not well characterized, and regulation of the pathway is not fully understood. With more than 22 capsaicinoids isolated from hot pepper, this genus provides an excellent example for exploring the evolution of secondary metabolites in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Bosland, P.W.E.J.V.P. Vegetable and Spice Capsicums (CABI, Wallingford, UK, 2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1666">2</a></sup>. Capsaicinoids have been found in nature to have antifungal and antibacterial properties, to act as a deterrent to animal predation when ingested and to have inherent properties that aid in avian seed dispersal. Capsaicinoids have many health benefits for humans: they are effective at inhibiting the growth of several forms of cancer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Mori, A. et al. Capsaicin, a component of red peppers, inhibits the growth of androgen-independent, p53 mutant prostate cancer cells. Cancer Res. 66, 3222â3229 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1671">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Surh, Y.J. More than spice: capsaicin in hot chili peppers makes tumor cells commit suicide. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 94, 1263â1265 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1674">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ito, K. et al. Induction of apoptosis in leukemic cells by homovanillic acid derivative, capsaicin, through oxidative stress: implication of phosphorylation of p53 at Ser-15 residue by reactive oxygen species. Cancer Res. 64, 1071â1078 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1677">10</a></sup>, are an analgesic for arthritis and other pain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Fraenkel, L., Bogardus, S.T. Jr., Concato, J. &amp; Wittink, D.R. Treatment options in knee osteoarthritis: the patient's perspective. Arch. Intern. Med. 164, 1299â1304 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1681">11</a></sup>, reduce appetite and promote weight loss<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Lejeune, M.P., Kovacs, E.M. &amp; Westerterp-Plantenga, M.S. Effect of capsaicin on substrate oxidation and weight maintenance after modest body-weight loss in human subjects. Br. J. Nutr. 90, 651â659 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1685">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Westerterp-Plantenga, M.S., Smeets, A. &amp; Lejeune, M.P.G. Sensory and gastrointestinal satiety effects of capsaicin on food intake. Int. J. Obes. (Lond.) 29, 682â688 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1688">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ludy, M.-J., Moore, G.E. &amp; Mattes, R.D. The effects of capsaicin and capsiate on energy balance: critical review and meta-analyses of studies in humans. Chem. Senses 37, 103â121 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1691">14</a></sup>. It is surprising that a complete understanding of the capsaicinoid pathway at the molecular level is lacking, considering the economic and cultural importance of capsaicinoids.</p><p>Here we report a high-quality genome sequence for hot pepper. <i>C. annuum</i> cv. CM334 (Criollo de Morelos 334), a landrace collected from the Mexican state of Morelos, has consistently exhibited high levels of resistance to diverse pathogens, including <i>Phytophthora capsici</i>, pepper mottle virus and root-knot nematodes. This landrace has been extensively used in hot pepper research and cultivar breeding. We also provide resequencing data for two cultivated peppers and for a wild species, <i>C. chinense</i>. Comparative genomics of members of the Solanaceae family, which includes hot pepper, provides an evolutionary view into the genome expansion, origin of pungency, distinct ripening process and disease resistance of hot pepper. This high-quality reference genome of hot pepper will serve as a platform for improving the horticultural, nutritional and medicinal values of <i>Capsicum</i> species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing, assembly and genetic variation</h3><p>We generated 650.2 Gb (186.6Ã genome coverage) of whole-genome shotgun sequence from <i>C. annuum</i> cv. CM334 (hereafter, CM334) by Illumina sequencing of genomic libraries with insert sizes ranging from 180 bp to 20 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 1â5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). On the basis of 19-mer analysis, we estimated the size of the genome to be 3.48 Gb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). For each library, we confirmed that raw data were unbiased by measuring the distribution of insert sizes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). After filtering, we assembled 3.06 Gb (87.9% of the 3.48-Gb total) into 37,989 scaffolds (N50 = 2.47 Mb) using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1757">15</a></sup> and SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C.V., Jansen, H.J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578â579 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1761">16</a></sup>, and 90% of the genome assembly was contained in 1,276 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">4</a>). We validated the genome assembly using 27 BAC sequences from CM334: 26 BAC sequences were fully covered by a single or multiple scaffolds and showed identities of greater than 99.9% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 5</a>). To construct pseudomolecules, we established a high-density genetic map with 6,281 markers using 120 recombinant inbred lines derived from <i>C. annuum</i> cv. Perennial and <i>C. annuum</i> cv. Dempsey (hereafter, Perennial and Dempsey) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 6â9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). We anchored scaffolds to the high-density genetic map (4,562 markers) and to the previously reported genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Yarnes, S.C. et al. Identification of QTLs for capsaicinoids, fruit quality, and plant architectureârelated traits in an interspecific Capsicum RIL population. Genome 56, 61â74 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4304e1793">17</a></sup>. Overall, we anchored 86.0% of the assembly (2.63 Gb; 1,357 scaffolds) as 12 chromosome pseudomolecules and ordered them (75.6%; 1,048 scaffolds) on the basis of genetic distance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the hot pepper genome and gene annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.2877/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We performed resequencing of two pepper cultivars (Perennial and Dempsey) and <i>de novo</i> sequencing of a wild species (<i>C. chinense</i> PI159236; hereafter, <i>C. chinense</i>) to provide a comprehensive overview of genetic variation and differences in genome structure among pepper cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">10â19</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). The proportion of the genome that was divergent between CM334 and the three other pepper genomes was 0.35, 0.39 and 1.85% (10.9, 11.9 and 56.6 million SNPs for Perennial, Dempsey and <i>C. chinense</i>, respectively) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 11</a>). Divergent sequences were widely dispersed along the pepper chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">13</a>). The number of low-coverage blocks (190 with 500-kb windows) that were divergent between <i>C. annuum</i> and <i>C. chinense</i> shows the genomic variation in the two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 16</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genomic landscape of pepper chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genomic landscape of pepper chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="572"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Left to right: density of matched blocks, gene density, repeat coverage and SNP density. Density of matched blocks is presented for <i>C. chinense</i>, Dempsey and Perennial (left to right) for 500-kb windows. Gene density is presented as the number of genes within 1-Mb intervals. Coverage by repeats represents the proportion of total TEs, <i>Gypsy</i> elements and <i>Copia</i> elements of LTRs within 1-Mb intervals. SNP density is presented as the number of SNPs per 1-Mb interval.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Transposable elements (TEs) have multiple roles in driving genome evolution in eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Feschotte, C., Jiang, N. &amp; Wessler, S.R. Plant transposable elements: where genetics meets genomics. Nat. Rev. Genet. 3, 329â341 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2099">18</a></sup>. In total, we identified 2.34 and 2.35 Gb (76.4 and 79.6%, respectively) of sequence in the assembled CM334 and <i>C. chinense</i> genomes as TEs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 20</a>). The predominant type of TE was long terminal repeat (LTR) elements, which represented approximately 1.7 Gb (more than 70%) of the total number of TEs in the two genomes. Most of the LTRs were <i>Gypsy</i> elements, which accounted for 67.0 and 62.1% of TEs in CM334 and <i>C. chinense</i>, respectively. A large number of Caulimoviridae elements were unique to either pepper genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 20</a>). The TEs were widely dispersed throughout the pepper genome and often led to the conversion of euchromatin into heterochromatin. The distribution of TEs was inversely correlated with gene density (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene prediction, gene annotation and RNA sequencing</h3><p>A total of 34,903 protein-coding genes were predicted in the PGA pipeline (Pepper Genome Annotation v. 1.5) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 21â28</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). This gene number is approximately the same as for tomato (International Tomato Annotation Group (iTAG) v2.3; 34,771 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2149">19</a></sup> and potato (Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium (PGSC) v3.4; 39,031 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2153">20</a></sup>, which suggests a similar gene number in Solanaceae plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">14</a>). We evaluated consensus gene models using 19.8 Gb of Illumina RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data. Overall, 93.2% of the predicted coding sequences were supported by Illumina data, demonstrating the high accuracy of gene prediction by PGA. To validate and improve gene models, we manually curated inaccurately annotated genes: 335 genes were manually added, and 86 genes were reclassified as pseudogenes. This manual inspection and curation resulted in the replacement of 1,789 genes with better gene models.</p><p>We performed genome-wide analysis of small RNAs and identified 177 microRNAs corresponding to 37 microRNA families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 26</a>). The distribution of small RNAs correlated well with gene density in the hot pepper genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>), similar to in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2172">20</a></sup> but in contrast to what is observed in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>.</p><p>In total, we identified 17,397 orthologous gene sets by comparison of the pepper and tomato genomes. To compare gene expression in the pepper and tomato genomes, we performed RNA-seq analyses of the placenta and pericarp at seven crucial stages of fruit development and compared gene expression in other tissues from these two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 22</a>). This tissue-by-tissue analysis showed that a significant change in gene expression patterns of orthologous genes (adjusted <i>P</i> value &lt; 0.01) occurred in 8.8% of the orthologous gene sets in leaf tissue and in 46.4% of the orthologous gene sets in pericarp tissue at 35 d post-anthesis (d.p.a.) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome expansion</h3><p>The hot pepper genome shared highly conserved syntenic blocks with the genome of tomato, its closest relative within the Solanaceae family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). However, the hot pepper genome was approximately fourfold larger than the tomato genome, owing to a greater accumulation of repetitive sequences in both heterochromatic and euchromatic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). The most common repeats in the hot pepper genome were LTR retrotransposons, as in many other plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Feschotte, C., Jiang, N. &amp; Wessler, S.R. Plant transposable elements: where genetics meets genomics. Nat. Rev. Genet. 3, 329â341 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2214">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2217">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711â716 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2220">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature 491, 705â710 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2223">23</a></sup>. However, the composition of LTR retrotransposons in the hot pepper genome was distinct from that for other plants. We estimated the total number of LTR retrotransposons by counting the reverse-transcriptase (RT) domains encoded by the hot pepper and tomato genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Of the RT domains encoded by the hot pepper genome, there were 12-fold more from the <i>Gypsy</i> family than from the <i>Copia</i> family, in contrast to the relative numbers observed for other plant genomes such as tomato, maize and barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2237">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2240">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium. A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature 491, 711â716 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2243">22</a></sup>. Therefore, substantial proliferation of the <i>Gypsy</i> family was the main cause of expansion of the hot pepper genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Analysis of pepper genome expansion compared to the tomato genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Analysis of pepper genome expansion compared to the tomato genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="405"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Circular diagram showing genetic collinearity between hot pepper and tomato. Hot pepper and corresponding tomato chromosomes are represented by red and blue bars, respectively. Lines link the positions of orthologous gene sets, with line color representing each chromosome set. (<b>b</b>) Top, linear comparison of chromosome 2 in hot pepper (upper bar) and tomato (lower bar). The positions of orthologous gene sets are indicated by lines linking the two bars. Blue and white blocks on the bars indicate repeat and genic regions, respectively. Bottom, comparisons of magnified heterochromatic and euchromatic regions in hot pepper and tomato. The positions of the magnified heterochromatic (Î±, Î±â²) and euchromatic (Î², Î²â²) regions are indicated above. (<b>c</b>) Comparison of copy numbers for <i>Gypsy</i>, <i>Copia</i> and Caulimoviridae elements. The fraction of each repeat element is indicated in parentheses. (<b>d</b>) Distribution of repeat elements on chromosome 10 in hot pepper and tomato. The graphs above and below each bar show repeat and gene densities, respectively. Data are shown for three subgroups of <i>Gypsy</i> elements, <i>Copia</i> and Caulimoviridae in hot pepper and for all subgroups in tomato. (<b>e</b>) Histogram of hot pepper and tomato LTR retrotransposon insertions. The insertion patterns of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> elements in each species are shown. The vertical gray bar indicates speciation time (19.1 million years ago). (<b>f</b>) Comparison of Caulimoviridae element composition. Phylogenetic trees are shown for hot pepper and tomato Caulimoviridae elements. Common elements and those existing in only one species are depicted by filled and empty triangles, respectively. The null subgroup is depicted by lines.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Of the <i>Gypsy</i> family elements, 83.5% were from the <i>Del</i> subgroup, and these elements accumulated primarily in heterochromatic regions of the hot pepper genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">19</a>). <i>Del</i> elements are known to selectively accumulate in heterochromatic regions owing to the function of the encoded chromodomain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Gao, X., Hou, Y., Ebina, H., Levin, H.L. &amp; Voytas, D.F. Chromodomains direct integration of retrotransposons to heterochromatin. Genome Res. 18, 359â369 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2330">24</a></sup>. However, we often found these <i>Del</i> elements in the collinear regions of the hot pepper genome that correlated with tomato euchromatin, with the insertion of these elements resulting in the formation of heterochromatic gene islands in the hot pepper genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The insertion pattern of <i>Del</i> elements may indicate that the hot pepper genome expanded by increasing the size of the existing heterochromatin and converting euchromatin into heterochromatin. We also observed that the <i>Tat</i> subgroup of the <i>Gypsy</i> family had selectively accumulated in euchromatic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>). The accumulation of <i>Copia</i> and <i>Tat</i> elements resulted in the expansion of hot pepper euchromatin.</p><p>We estimated the times of insertion for <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> elements using the method described by SanMiguel <i>et al.</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B.S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43â45 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2370">25</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>). The speciation time of pepper and tomato was reported as 19.1 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Wang, Y. et al. Euchromatin and pericentromeric heterochromatin: comparative composition in the tomato genome. Genetics 172, 2529â2540 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2381">26</a></sup>. Speciation time can be estimated from the peak value in frequency analysis of the synonymous substitution rate (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) of orthologous gene sets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cannon, S.B. et al. Legume genome evolution viewed through the Medicago truncatula and Lotus japonicus genomes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 14959â14964 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2389">27</a></sup>. Therefore, we analyzed a histogram of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values from 17,397 orthologous gene sets in hot pepper and tomato. The peak value of the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> frequency used to determined the speciation time point was observed at 0.3 (19.1 million years ago) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>). <i>Gypsy</i> elements in the hot pepper genome were gradually accumulated before speciation and peaked in frequency at a substitution value of 0.2 (12.7 million years ago) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2e</a>). <i>Copia</i> elements showed relatively recent insertion during the period corresponding to substitution values of between 0 and 0.2, which coincides with the insertion of <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia</i> elements in the tomato genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2e</a>). Variations in heterochromatin can create species barriers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Hughes, S.E. &amp; Hawley, R.S. Heterochromatin: a rapidly evolving species barrier. PLoS Biol. 7, e1000233 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2424">28</a></sup>. Thus, the unequal accumulation of <i>Gypsy</i> elements in heterochromatic regions of the progenitor species may have had a role in the speciation of hot pepper.</p><p>Among the RT domains encoded by the hot pepper genome, the RT domains of Caulimoviridae were unusually abundant (4.9%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>). The number of Caulimoviridae RT domains in hot pepper was 4,304, 9.2-fold more than that observed in tomato. Caulimoviridae is a DNA pararetrovirus of <span class="stix">â¼</span>8-kb unit length that evolved from a <i>Gypsy</i> element and replicates via an RNA intermediate without LTR sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Llorens, C. et al. The Gypsy Database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D70âD74 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2440">29</a></sup>. So far, Caulimoviridae elements have not been reported in repeat classification in other plant genome sequences, except for a small copy number in the banana genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213â217 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2444">30</a></sup>. We identified three subgroups of Caulimoviridae including Petuvirus, Caulimovirus and Cavemovirus in the hot pepper genome, but only Cavemovirus was identified in the tomato genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2f</a>). This finding indicates that the proliferation of Petuvirus and Caulimovirus elements resulted in the high abundance of Caulimoviridae in the hot pepper genome with random distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). Therefore, the accumulation of these elements might also have had a role in the expansion of the hot pepper genome in both heterochromatic and euchromatic regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Evolution of the capsaicin biosynthetic pathway</h3><p>Capsaicinoids are the determinants of pepper pungency. They are specialized secondary metabolites found only in <i>Capsicum</i> species. Capsaicinoids are synthesized by capsaicin synthase (<i>CS</i> and <i>Pun1</i>), which condenses vanillylamine from the phenylpropanoid pathway with 8-methyl-6-nonenoyl-CoA from the branched-chain fatty-acid pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Bennett, D.J. &amp; Kirby, G.W. Constitution and biosynthesis of capsaicin. J. Chem. Soc. C 1968, 442â446 (1968)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2475">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Leete, E. &amp; Louden, M.C.L. Biosynthesis of capsaicin and dihydrocapsaicin in Capsicum frutescens. J. Am. Chem. Soc. 90, 6837â6841 (1968)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2478">32</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Although the biosynthetic genes have been partly elucidated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Stewart, C. et al. The Pun1 gene for pungency in pepper encodes a putative acyltransferase. Plant J. 42, 675â688 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2486">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="del Rosario Abraham-JuÃ¡rez, M., Carmen Rocha-Granados, M., LÃ³pez, M., Rivera-Bustamante, R. &amp; Ochoa-Alejo, N. Virus-induced silencing of Comt, pAmt and Kas genes results in a reduction of capsaicinoid accumulation in chili pepper fruits. Planta 227, 681â695 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2489">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Mazourek, M. et al. A dynamic interface for capsaicinoid systems biology. Plant Physiol. 150, 1806â1821 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2492">35</a></sup>, the biochemical reactions, evolution and regulation of capsaicinoid biosynthesis are still largely unknown.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolution of the capsaicinoid biosynthetic pathway."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Evolution of the capsaicinoid biosynthetic pathway.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="509"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Capsaicinoid biosynthetic pathway. Named enzymes marked by red arrows correspond with the genes used in the analysis. (<b>b</b>) Comparison of transcriptional profiles for capsaicinoid biosynthetic genes. Heat maps show log<sub>2</sub>-scaled reads per kilobase per million reads (RPKM) for biosynthetic genes in CM334 (pungent pepper) and ECW (non-pungent pepper) and for their tomato orthologs. Tissues synthesizing capsaicinoid are indicated by asterisks. PAL, phenylalanine ammonia-lyase; C4H, cinnamate 4-hydroxylase; 4CL, 4-coumaroyl-CoA ligase; HCT, hydroxycinnamoyl transferase; C3H, p-coumaroyl shikimate/quinate 3-hydroxylase; COMT, caffeoyl-CoA 3-O-methyltransferase; HCHL, hydroxycinnamoyl-CoA hydratase lyase; AMT, aminotransferase; BCAT, branched-chain amino acid aminotransferase; Kas, ketoacyl-ACP synthase; ACL, acyl carrier protein; FatA, acyl-ACP thioesterase; CS, capsaicin synthase. Three biological replicates from pooled tissues were prepared for RNA-seq. (<b>c</b>) Microsynteny analysis of the hot pepper sequence containing <i>CS</i> (encoding capsaicin synthase; upper bar) and its collinear tomato sequence (lower bar). Lines linking the two bars indicate regions with &gt;70% similarity. <i>CS</i> paralogs and their corresponding genes in tomato are marked by arrows. Numbers above the arrows and letters below the arrows indicate multiplied paralogs. Black and red arrows indicate different origins for the paralogs. (<b>d</b>,<b>e</b>) Models of multiple gene duplications for <i>CS</i> paralogs (<b>d</b>) and their corresponding genes in tomato (<b>e</b>). Branch length in each phylogenetic tree is proportional to the synonymous substitution rate. The vertical gray bar on each tree indicates speciation time. Î± and Î² indicate the ancestral genes of the paralogs. Î² with serial numbers indicate duplicated ancestral genes. Reconstructed duplication events for the paralogs are shown to the right of each tree. Black and red boxes indicate different origins for the paralogs. Solid and dashed lines indicate duplication and translocation events, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using homology, microsynteny and previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Mazourek, M. et al. A dynamic interface for capsaicinoid systems biology. Plant Physiol. 150, 1806â1821 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2552">35</a></sup>, we identified all orthologous genes of the capsaicinoid pathway in the tomato genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). In a comparative transcriptome analysis, several genes in the pathway clearly showed differential expression in pepper and tomato fruits (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 29â31</a>). Fruit-specific expression of <i>CS</i>, encoding a homolog of acyltransferase, primarily occurred during pepper placenta development (at 16 d.p.a., 25 d.p.a. and mature green (MG)). All other genes in the pathway were also expressed at this stage, and capsaicinoids were synthesized in the placenta throughout this period<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Fujiwake, H., Suzuki, T. &amp; Iwai, K. Intracellular localization of capsaicin and its analogues in Capsicum fruit. II. The vacuole as the intracellular accumulation site of capsaicinoid in the protoplast of Capsicum fruit. Plant Cell Physiol. 21, 1023â1030 (1980)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2572">36</a></sup>. In contrast, the orthologous genes in the tomato pathway (<i>BCAT</i>, <i>Kas</i> and <i>CS</i>) were rarely expressed at this stage, and we obtained a similar result for the potato genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 24</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">33</a>). These results may indicate that changes in the gene expression of <i>BCAT</i>, <i>Kas</i> and <i>CS</i> enabled capsaicinoid synthesis in hot pepper fruits.</p><p>Genome-wide or local gene duplication is crucial for the origin of new gene functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Roth, C. et al. Evolution after gene duplication: models, mechanisms, sequences, systems, and organisms. J. Exp. Zool. B Mol. Dev. Evol. 308, 58â73 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2607">37</a></sup>. Microsynteny analysis of the genomic regions surrounding <i>CS</i> in hot pepper (<span class="stix">â¼</span>436 kb) and tomato (<span class="stix">â¼</span>183 kb) identified acyltransferase gene clusters in both species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). Phylogenetic analysis of the acyltransferase gene family within these regions in hot pepper (seven copies) and tomato (four copies) showed that <i>CS</i> appeared after speciation through multiple gene duplications. The seven copies of <i>CS</i> in hot pepper underwent five rounds of unequal tandem duplication events, whereas the four copies of <i>CS</i> in tomato experienced two rounds of duplication events from the ancestral genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig3">Fig. 3d,e</a>). <i>CS</i> likely emerged only after the final round of gene duplication in the hot pepper genome. Two other genes (<i>Kas</i> and <i>COMT</i>) in the capsaicinoid biosynthetic pathway also underwent unequal gene duplication events similar to those for the orthologous genes in tomato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). The biochemical functions of the acyltransferases within both clusters have not been addressed; however, it seems that neofunctionalization occurred with respect to both gene expression and protein function, conferring a role for CS in capsaicinoid synthesis after recent gene duplication. These results provide substantial new insight into the origin of pungency in hot pepper.</p><p>We compared expression of the capsaicinoid biosynthetic genes in the placentas of pungent and non-pungent peppers. Non-pungent peppers have a large deletion in <i>CS</i> that spans the region from the promoter to the first exon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Stewart, C. et al. The Pun1 gene for pungency in pepper encodes a putative acyltransferase. Plant J. 42, 675â688 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2648">33</a></sup>. During placenta development, <i>CS</i> was highly expressed only in pungent pepper and was barely expressed in non-pungent pepper (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). All other genes in the capsaicinoid biosynthetic pathway showed similar expression, except for <i>BCAT</i>, <i>COMT</i> and <i>FatA</i> at 6 d.p.a. This result indicates that non-pungent pepper species appeared because of loss of <i>CS</i> expression without substantial changes in the expression of other genes in the biosynthetic pathway.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Gene family analysis</h3><p>The distribution of orthologous gene families in hot pepper, tomato, potato, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape and rice was defined using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2683">38</a></sup>. We identified 23,245 hot pepper genes in 16,345 families, with 7,826 families shared by all 6 species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 34â37</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). A total of 2,139 gene families were unique to Solanaceae plants, and 756 gene families were unique to hot pepper. The hot pepper genome shared 27, 51 and 20 gene families with <i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape and rice, respectively. Variations in family size were found in many hot pepper gene families. We found that gene families involved in disease resistance and cellular functions, such as cytochrome P450 and heat shock protein 70 genes, were significantly expanded in the hot pepper genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Figs. 26â45</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 38â52</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>We identified 2,153 transcription factors (6.25% of the total genes) and transcriptional regulators in 80 gene families. Some transcription factors included Solanaceae-specific subclasses, specifically in the ARF, AP2/ERF, WRKY and NAC families. These transcription factors may have unique functions in Solanaceae, such as defense responses. Nine transcription factor families had fewer genes (including the AP2/ERF family) compared with other plant genomes, and no transcription factor of the DBP family was found in the hot pepper genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 43</a>).</p><p>A total of 684 genes from the nucleotide-binding siteâleucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) family were significantly expanded in the pepper genome compared with the other plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Tables 38</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">39</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">41</a>). NBS-LRR proteins are identified primarily as disease-resistance genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="van Ooijen, G., van den Burg, H.A., Cornelissen, B.J. &amp; Takken, F.L. Structure and function of resistance proteins in solanaceous plants. Annu. Rev. Phytopathol. 45, 43â72 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2727">39</a></sup>. The hot pepper genome contained 636 non-TIR (Toll/interleukin-1 receptor)-type NBS-LRRs, a number significantly higher than the 525 non-TIR NBS-LRRs in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Goff, S.A. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296, 92â100 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2731">40</a></sup>. The number of TIR-type proteins in the hot pepper genome (48) was similar to that in potato (47) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 39</a>). More than half of the NBS-LRR subclasses in each Solanaceae genome were classified into 37 subclasses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 41</a>). Notably, the <i>Bs2</i> (bacterial spot resistance gene)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tai, T.H. et al. Expression of the Bs2 pepper gene confers resistance to bacterial spot disease in tomato. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 96, 14153â14158 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2745">41</a></sup>-containing subclass (82 genes) exhibited explosive expansion in the hot pepper genome compared to the tomato (3 genes) and potato (1 gene) genomes. This expansion might be a consequence of evolutionary events of tandem duplication resulting in preferential clustering of the genes on chromosome 9 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 26</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 42</a>). Expansion of NBS-coding genes in the hot pepper genome resulted in the loss of collinearity with tomato or potato in NBS-coding regions, whereas higher synteny was maintained between the NBS-coding regions of tomato and potato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>). Comparisons of hot pepper <i>R</i> genes among Solanaceae plants suggested that expansion and diversification of <i>R</i> genes have been involved in lineage-specific parallel evolution through unequal gene-duplication events, resulting in different gene repertoires even in closely related species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Comparative fruit ripening</h3><p>Fleshy fruits are physiologically classified into two groups: climacteric and non-climacteric. Climacteric fruits such as tomato and banana display increases in respiration rate and ethylene synthesis during ripening. Non-climacteric fruits such as pepper and strawberry exhibit neither a respiratory burst nor elevated ethylene production during ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Klee, H.J. &amp; Giovannoni, J.J. Genetics and control of tomato fruit ripening and quality attributes. Annu. Rev. Genet. 45, 41â59 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2773">42</a></sup>. Thus, pepper and tomato provide suitable models for comparisons of fruit ripening processes. Gene repertories related to fruit ripening in hot pepper and tomato are well conserved (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 53</a>), which suggests that a gene regulatory mechanism likely causes differentiation in fruit ripening. To identify conserved and differential regulatory mechanisms in hot pepper and tomato, we investigated orthologous regulatory genes previously identified in tomato ripening. Expression of transcription factor genes (<i>RIN</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. A MADS-box gene necessary for fruit ripening at the tomato RIPENING-INHIBITOR (RIN) locus. Science 296, 343â346 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2782">43</a></sup>, <i>TAGL1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. Fleshy fruit expansion and ripening are regulated by the tomato SHATTERPROOF gene TAGL1. Plant Cell 21, 3041â3062 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2788">44</a>) and <i>NOR</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Giovannoni, J. Molecular biology of fruit maturation and ripening. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 52, 725â749 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2795">45</a></sup>) and genes involved in ethylene signaling pathways (<i>NR</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wilkinson, J.Q., Lanahan, M.B., Yen, H.C., Giovannoni, J.J. &amp; Klee, H.J. An ethylene-inducible component of signal transduction encoded by NEVER-RIPE. Science 270, 1807â1809 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2801">46</a></sup>, <i>ETR4</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Tieman, D.M., Taylor, M.G., Ciardi, J.A. &amp; Klee, H.J. The tomato ethylene receptors NR and LeETR4 are negative regulators of ethylene response and exhibit functional compensation within a multigene family. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 5663â5668 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2807">47</a>), <i>EIN2</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Wang, J., Chen, G., Hu, Z. &amp; Chen, X. Cloning and characterization of the EIN2-homology gene LeEIN2 from tomato. DNA Seq. 18, 33â38 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2814">48</a>) and <i>EIL</i> families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Tieman, D.M., Ciardi, J.A., Taylor, M.G. &amp; Klee, H.J. Members of the tomato LeEIL (EIN3-like) gene family are functionally redundant and regulate ethylene responses throughout plant development. Plant J. 26, 47â58 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2821">49</a></sup>) was conserved during fruit ripening (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). In contrast, <i>CNR</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Manning, K. et al. A naturally occurring epigenetic mutation in a gene encoding an SBP-box transcription factor inhibits tomato fruit ripening. Nat. Genet. 38, 948â952 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2830">50</a></sup>, <i>Uniform</i> (<i>Golden-like 2</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Powell, A.L. et al. Uniform ripening encodes a Golden 2-like transcription factor regulating tomato fruit chloroplast development. Science 336, 1711â1715 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2841">51</a></sup> and <i>HB-1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lin, Z. et al. A tomato HD-Zip homeobox protein, LeHB-1, plays an important role in floral organogenesis and ripening. Plant J. 55, 301â310 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2847">52</a>) showed distinct expression patterns in hot pepper and tomato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). <i>CNR</i> was expressed at very low levels during pepper ripening, whereas it was expressed at high levels during tomato ripening. The major ethylene biosynthetic genes for tomato ripening, including <i>ACS2</i>, <i>ACS4</i> and <i>ACO1</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Barry, C.S. et al. Differential expression of the 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylate oxidase gene family of tomato. Plant J. 9, 525â535 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2866">53</a>), were expressed at very low levels during hot pepper ripening (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). Thus, the conservation and divergence of the transcription of these genes and their interactions may lead to qualitative and quantitative differences in the physiological phenomena underlying ripening.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Transcriptional divergence and conservation of ripening-related genes in hot pepper and tomato."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Transcriptional divergence and conservation of ripening-related genes in hot pepper and tomato.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2877/MediaObjects/41588_2014_Article_BFng2877_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="486"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Heat map of normalized RNA-seq data prepared from three biological replicates for genes involved in fruit ripening. SPL-CNR, SQUAMOSA promoter-binding protein-likeâ<i>colorless non-ripening</i>; GLK2, golden 2-like; HB-1, HD-Zip homeobox protein; ACS, ACC synthase; ACO, ACC oxidase; CCS, capsanthin-capsorubin synthase; CYC-B, chromoplast-specific lycopene Î²-cyclase; MADS-RIN, MADS-box transcription factorâ<i>ripening inhibitor</i>; TAGL1, tomato AGAMOUS-like 1; NAC-NOR, NAC transcription factorâ<i>non-ripening</i>; TDR4, tomato FRUITFULL homolog; NR, <i>never ripe</i>; ETR4, ethylene receptor homolog 4; EIN2, ethylene-insensitive; EIL, EIN-like; ERF6, ethylene responsive factor 6; PSY1, phytoene synthase 1. I, divergent gene expression; II, conserved gene expression. (<b>b</b>) Working model of the control of non-climacteric ripening in pepper. Blue and red boxes represent genes that are downregulated and upregulated in pepper, respectively. Orange boxes represent genes that show similar expression in pepper and tomato. Double lines indicate an orthologous relationship between pepper and tomato genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2877/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The major pigments in pepper fruits are capsanthin and capsorubin, which are pepper-specific carotenoids synthesized by capsanthin-capsorubin synthase (CCS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Hugueney, P. et al. Metabolism of cyclic carotenoids: a model for the alteration of this biosynthetic pathway in Capsicum annuum chromoplasts. Plant J. 8, 417â424 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2916">54</a></sup>. CCS exhibits lycopene Î²-cyclase activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Hugueney, P. et al. Metabolism of cyclic carotenoids: a model for the alteration of this biosynthetic pathway in Capsicum annuum chromoplasts. Plant J. 8, 417â424 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2920">54</a></sup> and has an orthologous relationship with chromoplast-specific lycopene Î²-cyclase (CYC-B)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Ronen, G., Carmel-Goren, L., Zamir, D. &amp; Hirschberg, J. An alternative pathway to Î²-carotene formation in plant chromoplasts discovered by map-based cloning of Î² and old-gold color mutations in tomato. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 97, 11102â11107 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2924">55</a></sup>, which exhibits ethylene-dependent repression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Vrebalov, J. et al. Fleshy fruit expansion and ripening are regulated by the tomato SHATTERPROOF gene TAGL1. Plant Cell 21, 3041â3062 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2928">44</a></sup> during tomato ripening. <i>CCS</i> expression was extremely high during pepper ripening (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Fig4">Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 22</a>), which suggests that ethylene-dependent regulation may be preserved in both types of fruit ripening and lead to distinct outcomes. Therefore, these developmental and hormonal regulatory networks might be the main components that distinguish different ripening patterns.</p><p>One of the ripening characteristics distinguishing pepper and tomato is fruit softening, in which polygalacturonase (PG) has a central role. The hot pepper <i>PG</i> gene encoded a partial deletion of <span class="stix">â¼</span>90 amino acids in the C-terminal region of the protein compared to tomato <i>PG</i> (<i>LePG2a</i>, Solyc10g080210) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 46</a>).
