In comparative sequencing analysis of <i>PG</i> (CA10g18920) from wild-type pepper and the <i>Soft flesh</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Smith, P.G. Deciduous ripe fruit character in peppers. Proc. Am. Soc. Hort. Sci. 57, 343â344 (1951)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2963">56</a></sup> mutant, we found that a point mutation in the 3â² splice acceptor site at intron VIII generated a premature stop codon in the <i>PG</i> gene from wild-type pepper. The SNP in <i>PG</i> genetically cosegregated with the fruit softening phenotype and distinguished normal and soft-fleshed fruits among pepper germplasms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 47</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 54</a>). The levels of water-soluble pectin in the red fruit from the <i>Soft flesh</i> mutant were much higher than in the fruit from wild-type pepper (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 48</a>). The differential levels of water-soluble pectin likely supported PG-mediated pectin degradation and resultant fruit softening. Therefore, the impaired <i>PG</i> gene in wild-type hot pepper may have a pivotal role in the non-softening of fruit in coordination with transcriptional regulation of cell wallârelated genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 55</a>).</p><p>Ascorbate (vitamin C) is an essential nutrient for humans and acts as an antioxidant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Frei, B., England, L. &amp; Ames, B.N. Ascorbate is an outstanding antioxidant in human blood plasma. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 86, 6377â6381 (1989)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2995">57</a></sup>. Pepper fruit is one of the richest sources of ascorbate. The concentration of ascorbate in pepper is up to tenfold higher than in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wahyuni, Y., Ballester, A.R., Sudarmonowati, E., Bino, R.J. &amp; Bovy, A.G. Metabolite biodiversity in pepper (Capsicum) fruits of thirty-two diverse accessions: variation in health-related compounds and implications for breeding. Phytochemistry 72, 1358â1370 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d4304e2999">58</a></sup>. Most of the pepper genes in the <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-galactose pathway showed expression similar to or higher than in tomato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Table 56</a>). <i>GGP1</i>, which catalyzes the committed steps for <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-galactose synthesis, was highly expressed in all stages of pepper fruit development compared to in pepper vegetative tissues. The expression of pepper <i>GGP1</i> was two- to threefold higher during the green-fruit stages (at 6, 16 and 25 d.p.a.) compared to in tomato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Fig. 49</a>). These data indicate that the <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-galactose pathway may be the predominant biosynthetic pathway for ascorbate in hot pepper. Recycling is another factor that controls ascorbate content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Wang, Z., Xiao, Y., Chen, W., Tang, K. &amp; Zhang, L. Increased vitamin C content accompanied by an enhanced recycling pathway confers oxidative stress tolerance in Arabidopsis. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 52, 400â409 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3025">59</a></sup>. Ascorbate oxidases (APXs) generate dehydroascorbate; ascorbate can be regenerated by monodehydroascorbate reductase (MDHAR) and dehydroascorbate reductase (DHAR). <i>APX2</i> expression in tomato breaker fruits was 20-fold higher than in hot pepper. In contrast, <i>DHAR</i> was highly expressed during hot pepper ripening, with the highest expression observed at 16 d.p.a. for pepper fruits, at a level 5-fold higher than in tomato. These differentially expressed genes involved in ascorbate biosynthesis and recycling further explain the greater accumulation of ascorbate in pepper fruit.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>In 2011, the value of global hot pepper production was $14.4 billion, 40-fold higher than in 1980 (FAO statistics; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#Sec20">URLs</a>). Pepper consumption continues to grow because of this fruit's high nutritional value. The pepper genome sequences described here can serve as an important genomic resource for improving the nutritional and pharmaceutical value derived from hot pepper and for supporting evolutionary and comparative genomic studies of Solanaceae, one of the world's most diversified plant families. <i>Capsicum</i> is the only genus that evolved the biosynthesis of capsaicinoids, which consist of more than 20 related alkaloids that cause pungency in pepper fruit. The hot pepper genome sequence will provide an opportunity to gain a complete understanding of the capsaicinoid pathway and represents an excellent resource for exploring the evolution of secondary metabolites in plants. This study strongly suggests that pepper pungency originated through the evolution of new genes by unequal duplication of existing genes and owing to changes in gene expression in fruits after speciation. The hot pepper genome provides a strong foundation for further studies using comparative genomics, metabolic engineering and transgenic approaches to unveil the complete pathway of capsaicinoid biosynthesis in <i>Capsicum</i> species. In combination with the recently published tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3054">19</a></sup> and potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xu, X. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3058">20</a></sup> genomes, the hot pepper genome will elucidate the evolution, diversification and adaptation of more than 3,000 Solanaceae species, which are adapted to a wide range of geoecological habitats ranging from the driest deserts to tropical rainforests. Resequencing of two cultivars and <i>de novo</i> sequencing of <i>C. chinense</i> provides a landscape of genomic diversity among <i>Capsicum</i> species. The hot pepper genome will enable the advancement of new breeding technologies through the exploration of genome-wide associations and genomic selection studies on horticulturally important traits such as fruit size, yield, pungency, tolerance to abiotic stresses, nutritional content and resistance to multiple diseases.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11"><i>De novo</i> and resequencing of pepper genomes.</h3><p>A Mexican landrace, <i>C. annuum</i> cv. CM334, and a wild species, <i>C. chinense</i> PI159236, were used for <i>de novo</i> genome sequencing, and <i>C. annuum</i> cv. Perennial and <i>C. annuum</i> cv. Dempsey were resequenced. Paired-end and mate-pair libraries for sequencing were prepared with the corresponding kits (Illumina) following the manufacturer's instructions and were validated with KAPA SYBR FAST Master Mix Universal 2Ã qPCR Master Mix (Kapa Biosystems). Constructed libraries were sequenced on Illumina platforms (Genome Analyzer IIx and HiSeq 2000) using standard protocols (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly.</h3><p>Before genome assembly, short-read sequences from each library were preprocessed using in-house preprocessing pipelines to increase the accuracy of genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). Contamination from bacterial sequences, duplicated short reads and low-quality bases in each short-read sequence was removed. Preprocessed short reads were error corrected using Quake<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Kelley, D.R., Schatz, M.C. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Quake: quality-aware detection and correction of sequencing errors. Genome Biol. 11, R116 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3117">60</a></sup>. Remaining sequence was then assembled using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3121">15</a></sup> with the optimal <i>K</i>-mer for each library (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). The assembled RCM334 genome sequence was validated with 27 BACs with insert size larger than 70 kb from euchromatic or heterochromatic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). The <i>C. chinense</i> genome assembly was assessed using <i>C. chinense</i> ESTs and annotated CM334 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Construction of genetic linkage map and pseudomolecules.</h3><p>A high-density genetic map for hot pepper was constructed with 120 recombinant inbred lines (RILs) derived from an intraspecific cross between Dempsey and Perennial using SNP markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). Markers were then aligned to the scaffolds using BLASTN (identity â¥ 98% and coverage â¥ 70%).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Analysis of genomic variations.</h3><p>Preprocessed raw data for Perennial, Dempsey and <i>C. chinense</i> were mapped to the CM334 reference genome using Bowtie 2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357â359 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3165">61</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3172">62</a></sup> was used to call DNA variations. Classification and annotation of DNA variations was performed using SnpEff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly (Austin) 6, 80â92 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3176">63</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Transcriptome sequencing and analysis.</h3><p>CM334 plants were grown under standard conditions (day/night cycles, 27/19 Â°C, 16/8 h) in a greenhouse. Roots, leaves and stems were harvested from plants 6 weeks after sowing. Pepper pericarp and placenta tissues from CM334, pepper placenta from ECW and tomato placenta from <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> cv. Alisa Craig were harvested at 6 d.p.a., 16 d.p.a., 25 d.p.a., MG, B, B5 and B10. For transcriptome comparison, previously published RNA-seq data for tomato pericarp was used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3192">19</a></sup>. Three biological replicates from pooled tissues were prepared. Total RNA was isolated using TRIzol reagent (Invitrogen). A modified TruSeq method was used to construct a strand-specific RNA-seq library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Zhong, S. et al. High-throughput Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing library preparation. Cold Spring Harb. Protoc. 2011, 940â949 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3196">64</a></sup> with different index primers, and libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 system. Resulting reads were aligned to pepper CM334 sequences and tomato Heinz sequences using CLC Assembly Cell (CLC Bio). Counts for mapped reads were normalized by RPKM. Differentially expressed genes during pericarp development were identified using DESeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Anders, S. &amp; Huber, W. Differential expression analysis for sequence count data. Genome Biol. 11, R106 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3200">65</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome annotation.</h3><p>Genome annotation was performed using the PGA pipeline (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). This pipeline uses a combination of evidence-based gene prediction (RNA-seq and proteins) and <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction. Consensus gene models were determined by EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3221">66</a></sup>, and these models were then updated with PASA assembly alignments. Gene functions were assigned according to the best alignment attained using BLASTP to the UniProt database (including SWISS-PROT and TrEMBL databases) and INTERPRO scan.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Analysis of differential gene expression.</h3><p>Orthologous gene sets were found by reciprocal BLAST with pepper and tomato coding sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). Analysis of differential gene expression was carried out using DESeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Anders, S. &amp; Huber, W. Differential expression analysis for sequence count data. Genome Biol. 11, R106 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3236">65</a></sup>. Synonymous substitution rates for orthologous gene sets were calculated by codeml in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3240">67</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Repeat annotation and genome expansion analysis.</h3><p>All TE-related repeats were characterized using RepeatMasker with a custom library for pepper. Synonymous substitution rates for LTRs were calculated by codeml in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3252">67</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>). Visualization of comparative sequence analysis for pepper and tomato was performed with in-house Python scripts or the Circos program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639â1645 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3259">68</a></sup>. Phylogenetic trees were constructed using the MEGA5 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3263">69</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">OrthoMCL analysis.</h3><p>Orthologous gene clusters were assigned from OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2877#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4304e3275">38</a></sup> with its standard parameters of six species to identify gene families enriched in the hot pepper genome. Gene sets from hot pepper (PGAv1.0), tomato (v2.3), <i>Arabidopsis</i> (TAIR10), grape (VvGDB v2.0), rice (MSU RGAP 7) and potato (PGSC v3.4) were used to infer putative orthologous gene families. Splice variants and incomplete gene models in the genomes were removed, and an all-by-all comparison was then performed using BLASTP with an <i>E</i> value of 1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup>. A total of 161,775 protein sequences were clustered into 21,808 gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2877#MOESM15">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">URLs.</h3><p>Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations (FAO statistics), <a href="http://faostat.fao.org/">http://faostat.fao.org/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Accession codes.</h3><p>Whole-genome sequences for the pepper have been deposited in GenBank under accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=AYRZ00000000">AYRZ00000000</a> (the version described in the manuscript is the first version, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AYRZ01000000">AYRZ01000000</a>). Further information, including the CM334 genome assembly, pseudomolecules, annotations and <i>C. chinense</i> genome assembly are available through our website at <a href="http://peppergenome.snu.ac.kr">http://peppergenome.snu.ac.kr</a>.</p></div></div></section>

Y.D.C., B.-S.P. and Y.-H.L. coordinated the project. S.K., M.P., S.-I.Y., Y.-M.K., J.M.L., H.-A.L., B.-D.K., I.Y., Y.Y., R.W.K., I.-Y.C., B.-S.C., J.-S.L. and E.S. performed data generation and/or analysis and managed subprojects. Y.-M.K., H.-A.L., S.-B.K., H.-Y.L. and S.-Y. Kim prepared DNA and RNA samples. S.K. performed <i>de novo</i> genome sequencing and assembly. Y.-M.K. was genome-annotation coordinator, and E.S., G.-W.L., H.-Y.L., M.-S.K., Y.S., S.-J.N. and J.P. were involved in genome annotation. J.M.L. coordinated comparative fruit ripening analysis, and H.-A.L., J.J.G., B.-D.K. and I.Y. were involved in comparative fruit ripening analysis. M.P. coordinated genome and capsaicinoid biosynthetic pathway analysis. S.K., M.P., S.-I.Y., Y.-M.K., E.S., J.C., K.C., K.-T.K., K.J. and G.-W.L. performed bioinformatics analysis. B.-C.K., Y.D.J., H.-B.Y., H.-J.J., W.-H.K., S.-H.J., B.-W.L., A.v.D., H.A. and T.H. were involved in map construction and development. J.-K.K. performed FISH analysis. M.P., J.M.L., H.-A.L., A.v.D., H.A. and T.H. performed transcriptome data generation and/or analysis. S.-I.Y. coordinated gene family analysis; Y.-M.K., E.S., J.C., S.-K.O., C.B., J.H.H., J.-S.K., O.C., I.P., M.C.S., S.B.L., Y.-K.K., Y.S.S., S.-Y.
